                            <div class="player-info-box-header "><div class="player-info-box-name"><h1>Neil Walker</h1><div class="player-info-box-name-team">&nbsp;</div></div><div class="player-info-box-item">Age: 32</div><div class="player-info-box-item">Bats/Throws: B/R</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-item">6' 3" / 210</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-pos">2B</div></div>
                        <div class="player-info-bio">
                            <strong>Birthdate: </strong>9/10/1985 (32 y, 6 m)<br /><b>Drafted:</b> <a href="statss.aspx?playerid=7539&position=2B#draft" style="text-decoration:none;">2004 June Amateur Draft - Round: 1, Pick: 11, Overall: 11, Team: Pittsburgh Pirates</a><br /><strong>Contract:</strong> <a href="statss.aspx?playerid=7539&position=2B#contract" >$17.2M / 1 Years (2017)</a>
                        </div>
                    </td>

                    
                    <tr><td><hr class="player-page-info-sep"/><div class="player-page-rotowire"><strong><a href="https://www.rotowire.com/users/ad_jump.asp?id=688&playerid=8265">RotoWire News</a>:</strong> Baltimore is interested in signing Walker, but on a minor-league deal, Jon Heyman of FanRag Sports reports. He earlier rejected an offer of a minor-league deal from Kansas City, the Boston Globe reports. (3/8/2018)</div></td></tr>

        <td style="width: 302px; height: 250px; vertical-align: top;">
            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Walker surprised a lot of people in 2010. The former No. 1 draft pick had some disappointing minor league seasons and had one foot out the door before a solid 2010 showing in Triple-A gave him one last shot. Walker isn't going anywhere now and he's the odds-on favorite to start at second base for the Pirates in 2011. His value is still down a bit from when he was drafted, simply because he doesn't catch anymore. However, he will likely serve as the club's third-string catcher and an injury to one of the top two could increase Walker's fantasy value significantly depending on the games-played requirement in your fantasy league. He may already have third-base eligibility in some leagues after making six appearances there in 2010. Like fellow rookie Jose Tabata, Walker has the potential to hit .280-.300 but he'll hit for more power and could slug about 15 homers. He could potentially reach the 20-homer mark if his line drives (22.4%) get a little more loft. (Marc Hulet)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Walker could be a solid contributor at second base in NL-only leagues. If he plays some third base, his versatility could make him even more attractive and could end up on some benches in mixed leagues.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Neil Walker is an average second baseman. The 25-year-old has been fairly consistent in his first two seasons. He will likely put up low double-digit home runs, 80 runs and RBIs, a little less than 10 stolen bases and a .280 batting average. None of these stats are going to be enough to win a league, but they won't lose it either. If you are in the situation where you can afford to platoon at the position, Walker hits right-handers better. He hits .281/.342/.435 with a home run every 43 PAs vs righties and .276/.327/.391 and a home run every 76 PAs  vs lefties. Also he is fairly young and has the chance to develop some more power as he matures. He would be a good addition to a team that is set in the rest of their positions and needs to solidify second base. (Jeff Zimmerman)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Neil Walker is the average/boring second base candidate and sometimes that's all that you need.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>A former catching prospect, Neil Walker has turned himself into a serviceable defensive second baseman, and the 27-year-old’s offensive numbers fit right in with the rest of the league’s pivot men. Getting ready for his fourth season in the bigs, Walker is entering his prime years for fantasy production, and one could think that there’s more power to be had in his 6’3” frame. If the switch-hitter can crank 20 balls out of the yard, he’ll immediately become a top-10 fantasy second baseman. Even if Walker doesn’t improve, he’s still generally undervalued and worth having on your roster in any format. (Zach Sanders)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Neil Walker has become a solid fantasy second baseman, and he still has room to improve his power production. He should be drafted in all leagues, and he should be starting in most of them.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Since becoming a regular player in 2010, Walker has homered at least 12 times a season, and his 54 total home runs place him 11th among second basemen. He can't compete with the likes of Robinson Cano (117 homers) and Dan Uggla (110), but his number is respectable. Walker's .340 on-base percentage also places him 11th among qualified second basemen in that time frame, and his .426 slugging percentage places 10th. Such a consistent skill set is often underrated, and we see this when we boil it down to one number. His 112 wRC+ places him a bit better -- seventh. Most fantasy leagues don't use wRC+ in their scoring of course, which is why Walker tends to fall down draft boards. His career .273 batting average isn't exactly headline worthy, and he has never stolen 10 bases in a single season. In other words, he's not flashy. And he also has had some back problems in the past, and those sorts of injuries tend to not go away. But despite that Walker has produced, and there is little reason to think he will do otherwise in 2014. If you can land Robinson Cano or Dustin Pedroia or Jason Kipnis or Matt Carpenter, then do so, but if you can't, waiting things out and landing on Walker is definitely a good Plan B.<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>"Solid yet unspectacular" is a phrase that perfectly defines Walker -- he has for three years been a very reliable hitter, even if he doesn't excel in any one area. He should remain a vital cog in the Pirates' machine this season, and if you miss out on the top second basemen, Walker is definitely a great fallback option.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>After being the model of consistency for the first four full seasons of his career, the 29-year-old Walker flashed some power upside with 23 home runs in 2014 -- seven more than his previous best from 2013. Given his always-solid batting average, RBI, and run totals, that newfound power sneaks Walker up close to the top ten among second basemen. He probably still falls a bit short in that respect—mainly because of the lack of steals and the track record of power—but Walker remains as safe a middle infielder as you can find. (Scott Spratt)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Walker has been one of the most consistent middle infield options in baseball over the last five seasons, and his career high 23 home runs in 2014 offer some additional upside. He could potential reach the top ten among second basemen despite his lack of steals.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>At least Neil Walker is consistent. His league-average plate discipline, his mid-teens power, his love affair with the disabled list -- all consistent throughout his career. I sell him short on playing time, though; were it not for an appendectomy in 2014, Walker would have posted his second-straight fully healthy season. His injury history is legitimate, but a fluke injury obscures the picture a bit. Steamer took the bait, projecting him for a meager 526 plate appearances -- it would be his lowest total since 2010, when he was called up mid-season -- despite a high likelihood he will dominate reps at second baseman for the New York Metropolitans. It's a market inefficiency worth exploiting for those who think he can stay healthy -- and even if he doesn't, the injury downside is already built into his price. Admittedly, there's not a lot of profit to be had in 50-or-so extra plate appearances, but it's better than none. He projects to be drafted late among the top-20 second basemen but has back-end top-10 potential, making him a relatively risk-free dollar play in standard leagues and a thrifty signing in deeper formats. (Alex Chamberlain)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Neil Walker's injury history is legitimate but misleading, given he played all of 2015 and was sidelined in 2014 for an appendectomy -- in other words, because of a fluke. Yet the gloomy outlook on playing time has suppressed his value, making him a relatively risk-free bet to attain top-15 value at second base and a thrifty play in all formats.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Neil Walker had never hit more than 3 home runs against left handed pitching in a single season, and he hadn’t hit more than 2 since 2010. In 2016 he hit 8, and that explains his 2016 in a nutshell. In 2016, Walker flipped the script on what was otherwise a very consistent career. His home run rates climbed to career highs, especially against left handed pitchers which reached 17% of fly balls. Overall his ground ball and fly ball rates flipped flopped, turning him into a strong fly ball hitter while setting a career high FB% and a career low GB% - if you ignore his 2009 September cup of coffee. He went from a career 17% below average hitter against left handed pitching to batting and astonishing 68% above average against lefties. Unfortunately, his injury history caught up to him, and he underwent season ending back surgery in early September. Even with missing the final month of the season, he matched his career high 23 home runs and set a new career highs in walk rate and slugging percentage. He claimed his ailing back did little to limit his swing but did limit his ability to run the bases. His numbers appear to back that up, with a slight drop in doubles and stolen bases in addition to the previously stated power surge. (Andrew Perpetua)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Walker should be healthy going into 2017. Whether you buy his newfound penchant for destroying lefties or not, Walker is a top 15 second basemen. Don't be afraid to turn to him when the top tier second basemen come off the board.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

Then, just as Walker showed signs of breaking out of his funk, the Mets traded him to the Brewers. Though his fly ball rate continued to be relatively muted (38%, as opposed to his 43% mark for his Mets career), Walker finished respectably, batting .267 with four home runs and eight doubles in his 38 games as a Brewer. While those rates would have fit right in with his stats from his Pirate years, the rise in his walk rate was unmistakable. Walker had never registered a double-digit walk rate in a full season, but during his brief tenure in Milwaukee, he posted a 19% rate. Walker had already shown improvements in plate discipline earlier in the year with the Mets, but with the Brewers, only 40% of the pitches he saw were in the strike zone. That combination enabled Walker to live up to his name and compile a .409 OBP after the trade. However, as Walker had struggled against lefties (.298 slugging percentage in 2017), Brewers manager Craig Counsell opted let him bat only 16 times versus southpaws.             <td align="right" style="width: 805px">
